      <p><b>George Lansbury</b> (21 February 1859&nbsp;– 7 May 1940) was a British politician and <a href="/wiki/Reform_movement" title="Reform movement">social reformer</a> who led the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> from 1932 to 1935. Apart from a brief period of ministerial office during the <a href="/wiki/Second_MacDonald_ministry" title="Second MacDonald ministry">Labour government of 1929–31</a>, he spent his political life campaigning against established authority and vested interests, his main causes being the promotion of <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a> and world disarmament. </p>
      <p>Originally a <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">radical Liberal</a>, Lansbury became a socialist in the early 1890s, and thereafter served his local community in the <a href="/wiki/East_End_of_London" title="East End of London">East End of London</a> in numerous elective offices. His activities were underpinned by his Christian beliefs which, except for a short period of doubt, sustained him through his life. Elected to <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament</a> in 1910, he resigned his seat in 1912 to campaign for women's suffrage, and was briefly imprisoned after publicly supporting militant action. </p>
      <p>In 1912, Lansbury helped to establish the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Herald_(UK_newspaper)" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Herald (UK newspaper)">Daily Herald</a></i> newspaper, and became its editor. Throughout the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a> the paper maintained a strongly pacifist stance, and supported the October 1917 <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a>. These positions contributed to Lansbury's failure to be elected to Parliament in 1918. He devoted himself to local politics in his home borough of <a href="/wiki/Poplar,_London" title="Poplar, London">Poplar</a>, and went to prison with 30 fellow-councillors for his part in the <a href="/wiki/Poplar_Rates_Rebellion" title="Poplar Rates Rebellion">Poplar "rates revolt"</a> of 1921. </p>
      <p>After his return to Parliament in 1922, Lansbury was denied office in the brief <a href="/wiki/First_MacDonald_ministry" title="First MacDonald ministry">Labour government of 1924</a>, although he served as <a href="/wiki/First_Commissioner_of_Works" title="First Commissioner of Works">First Commissioner of Works</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Second_MacDonald_ministry" title="Second MacDonald ministry">Labour government of 1929–31</a>. After the political and economic crisis of August 1931, Lansbury did not follow his leader, <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a>, into the <a href="/wiki/First_National_Ministry" class="mw-redirect" title="First National Ministry">National Government</a>, but remained with the Labour Party. As the most senior of the small contingent of Labour MPs that survived the <a href="/wiki/1931_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1931 United Kingdom general election">1931 general election</a>, Lansbury became the Leader of the Labour Party. His <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifism</a> and his opposition to rearmament in the face of rising European <a href="/wiki/Fascism_in_Europe" title="Fascism in Europe">fascism</a> put him at odds with his party, and when his position was rejected at the 1935 Labour Party conference, he resigned the leadership. He spent his final years travelling through the United States and Europe in the cause of peace and disarmament. </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_End_upbringing">East End upbringing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: East End upbringing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Plaque_on_home_of_George_Lansbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_65869.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Plaque_on_home_of_George_Lansbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_65869.jpg/170px-Plaque_on_home_of_George_Lansbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_65869.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Plaque_on_home_of_George_Lansbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_65869.jpg/255px-Plaque_on_home_of_George_Lansbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_65869.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Plaque_on_home_of_George_Lansbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_65869.jpg/340px-Plaque_on_home_of_George_Lansbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_65869.jpg 2x" data-file-width="564" data-file-height="563"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Plaque_on_home_of_George_Lansbury_-_geograph.org.uk_-_65869.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Plaque on the assumed birthplace of George Lansbury in Haleswoth, Suffolk. It incorrectly records Lansbury's death year as 1947.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>George Lansbury was born in <a href="/wiki/Halesworth" title="Halesworth">Halesworth</a> in the county of <a href="/wiki/Suffolk" title="Suffolk">Suffolk</a> on 22 February 1859.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[n 1]</a></sup> He was the third of nine children born to a railway worker, also named George Lansbury, and Anne Lansbury (née Ferris).<sup id="cite_ref-ODNB_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-5">[4]</a></sup> George senior's job involved the supervision of railway construction gangs; the family was often on the move, and living conditions were primitive.<sup id="cite_ref-P3_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P3-2">[2]</a></sup> Through his progressive-minded mother and grandmother, young George became familiar with the names of great contemporary reformers—<a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">Gladstone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cobden" title="Richard Cobden">Richard Cobden</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Bright" title="John Bright">John Bright</a>—and began to read the radical <i><a href="/wiki/Reynolds_News" class="mw-redirect" title="Reynolds News">Reynolds's Newspaper</a></i>. By the end of 1868, the family had moved into London's East End, the district in which Lansbury would live and work for almost all his life.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The essayist <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Blythe" title="Ronald Blythe">Ronald Blythe</a> has described the East End of the 1860s and 1870s as "stridently English&nbsp;... The smoke-blackened streets were packed with illiterate multitudes [who] stayed alive through sheer birdlike ebullience".<sup id="cite_ref-Blythe272_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blythe272-7">[6]</a></sup> Interspersed with spells of work, Lansbury attended schools in <a href="/wiki/Bethnal_Green" title="Bethnal Green">Bethnal Green</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whitechapel" title="Whitechapel">Whitechapel</a>. He then held a succession of manual jobs, including work as a coaling contractor in partnership with his elder brother, James, loading and unloading coal wagons. This was heavy and dangerous work, and led to at least one near-fatal accident.<sup id="cite_ref-S8_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S8-8">[7]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During his adolescence and early adulthood, Lansbury was a regular attender at the public gallery at the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons" title="House of Commons">House of Commons</a>, where he heard and remembered many of Gladstone's speeches on the main foreign policy issue of the day, the "<a href="/wiki/Eastern_Question" title="Eastern Question">Eastern Question</a>". He was present at the riots which erupted outside Gladstone's house on 24 February 1878 after a peace meeting in <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park_(London)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hyde Park (London)">Hyde Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> Shepherd writes that Gladstone's Liberalism, proclaiming liberty, freedom and community interests was "a heady mix that left an indelible mark" on the youthful Lansbury.<sup id="cite_ref-S10_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S10-10">[9]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>George Lansbury senior died in 1875. That year young George met fourteen-year-old Elizabeth Brine, whose father Isaac Brine owned a local sawmill. The couple eventually married in 1880, at Whitechapel parish church, where the vicar, J. Franklin Kitto, had been Lansbury's spiritual guide and counsellor. Apart from a period of doubt in the 1890s when he temporarily rejected the Church, Lansbury remained a staunch <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a> until his death.<sup id="cite_ref-P13_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P13-11">[10]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:StateLibQld_1_110096_Drawing_of_migrants_disembarking_from_a_ship,_ca._1885.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/StateLibQld_1_110096_Drawing_of_migrants_disembarking_from_a_ship%2C_ca._1885.jpg/170px-StateLibQld_1_110096_Drawing_of_migrants_disembarking_from_a_ship%2C_ca._1885.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/StateLibQld_1_110096_Drawing_of_migrants_disembarking_from_a_ship%2C_ca._1885.jpg/255px-StateLibQld_1_110096_Drawing_of_migrants_disembarking_from_a_ship%2C_ca._1885.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/StateLibQld_1_110096_Drawing_of_migrants_disembarking_from_a_ship%2C_ca._1885.jpg/340px-StateLibQld_1_110096_Drawing_of_migrants_disembarking_from_a_ship%2C_ca._1885.jpg 2x" data-file-width="713" data-file-height="1000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:StateLibQld_1_110096_Drawing_of_migrants_disembarking_from_a_ship,_ca._1885.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Migrants disembarking from a ship in Brisbane, c. 1885
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1881, the first of Lansbury's twelve children, Bessie, was born; another daughter, Annie, followed in 1882. Seeking to improve his family's prospects, Lansbury decided that their best hopes of prosperity lay in emigrating to Australia. The London agent-general for <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a> depicted a land of boundless opportunities, with work for all; seduced by this appeal, Lansbury and Bessie raised the necessary passage money, and in May 1884 set sail with their children for <a href="/wiki/Brisbane" title="Brisbane">Brisbane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-S10_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S10-10">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-P22_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P22-12">[11]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On the outward passage, the family experienced illness, discomfort and danger; on one occasion the ship came close to foundering during a <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-P22_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P22-12">[11]</a></sup> On arrival at <a href="/wiki/Brisbane" title="Brisbane">Brisbane</a> in July 1884, Lansbury found that contrary to the London agent's promises, there was a superfluity of labour and work was hard to come by. His first job, breaking stone, proved to be too physically punishing; he moved to a better-paid position as a van driver, but was sacked when, for religious reasons, he refused to work on Sundays.<sup id="cite_ref-P24_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P24-13">[12]</a></sup> He then contracted to work on a farm some 80 miles inland, to find upon arrival that his employer had misled him about living conditions and terms of employment.<sup id="cite_ref-S13_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S13-14">[13]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>For several months, the Lansbury family lived in extreme squalor before Lansbury secured release from the contract. Back in Brisbane, he worked for a while at the newly built Brisbane cricket ground. As a keen follower of the game he hoped to see the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Test_cricket_from_1884_to_1889#Lillywhite,_Shaw_and_Shrewsbury's_second_tour_1884/5" title="History of Test cricket from 1884 to 1889">visiting English touring team</a> play but, as Lansbury's biographer <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Postgate" title="Raymond Postgate">Raymond Postgate</a> records, "he learned that cricket watching was not a pleasure for workmen".<sup id="cite_ref-P24_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P24-13">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[n 2]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Throughout his tenure in Australia, Lansbury sent letters home, revealing the truth about conditions facing immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-P24_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P24-13">[12]</a></sup> To a friend he wrote in March 1885: "Mechanics are <i>not</i> wanted. Farm labourers are <i>not</i> wanted&nbsp;... Hundreds of men and women are not able to get work&nbsp;... The streets are foul day and night, and if I had a sister I would <i>shoot her dead</i> rather than see her brought out to this little hell on earth".<sup id="cite_ref-S13_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S13-14">[13]</a></sup> In May 1885, having received from Isaac Brine sufficient funds for a passage home, the Lansbury family left Australia for good.<sup id="cite_ref-P24_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P24-13">[12]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Radical_Liberal">Radical Liberal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Radical Liberal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_campaigns">First campaigns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: First campaigns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>On his return to London, Lansbury took a job in Brine's timber business. In his spare time he campaigned against the false prospectuses offered by colonial emigration agents. His speech at an emigration conference at <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_London" title="King's College London">King's College</a> in London in April 1886 impressed delegates; shortly afterwards, the government established an Emigration Information Bureau under the <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Office" title="Colonial Office">Colonial Office</a>. This body was required to provide accurate information on the state of labour markets in all the government's overseas possessions.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Having joined the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> shortly after his return from Australia, Lansbury became first a ward secretary and then general secretary for the Bow and Bromley Liberal and Radical Association.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> His effective campaigning skills had been noted by leading Liberals, including <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Montagu,_1st_Baron_Swaythling" title="Samuel Montagu, 1st Baron Swaythling">Samuel Montagu</a>, the Liberal <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament">MP</a> for <a href="/wiki/Whitechapel_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Whitechapel (UK Parliament constituency)">Whitechapel</a>, who persuaded the young activist to be his agent in the <a href="/wiki/1885_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1885 United Kingdom general election">1885 general election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-S19_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S19-19">[17]</a></sup> Lansbury's handling of this election campaign prompted Montagu to urge him to stand for parliament himself.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> Lansbury declined this, partly on practical grounds (MPs were then unpaid and he had to provide for his family), and partly on principle; he was becoming increasingly convinced that his future lay not as a radical Liberal but as a <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-S19_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S19-19">[17]</a></sup> He continued to serve the Liberals, as an agent and local secretary, while expressing his socialism in a short-lived monthly radical journal, <i>Coming Times</i>, which he founded and co-edited with a fellow-dissident, William Hoffman.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="London_County_Council_elections.2C_1889"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="London_County_Council_elections,_1889">London County Council elections, 1889</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: London County Council elections, 1889">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Metropolitan_Board_of_Works_in_Spring_Gardens_1860_ILN.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Metropolitan_Board_of_Works_in_Spring_Gardens_1860_ILN.jpg/220px-Metropolitan_Board_of_Works_in_Spring_Gardens_1860_ILN.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Metropolitan_Board_of_Works_in_Spring_Gardens_1860_ILN.jpg/330px-Metropolitan_Board_of_Works_in_Spring_Gardens_1860_ILN.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Metropolitan_Board_of_Works_in_Spring_Gardens_1860_ILN.jpg/440px-Metropolitan_Board_of_Works_in_Spring_Gardens_1860_ILN.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="441"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Metropolitan_Board_of_Works_in_Spring_Gardens_1860_ILN.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Metropolitan Board of Works building in 
         <a href="/wiki/Spring_Gardens" title="Spring Gardens">Spring Gardens</a> near 
         <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a>, original headquarters of the London County Council
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1888 Lansbury agreed to act as election agent for <a href="/wiki/Jane_Cobden" title="Jane Cobden">Jane Cobden</a>, who was contesting the first elections for the newly formed <a href="/wiki/London_County_Council" title="London County Council">London County Council</a> (LCC) as Liberal candidate for the Bow and Bromley division.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> Cobden, an early supporter of women's suffrage, was the fourth child of the Victorian radical statesman <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cobden" title="Richard Cobden">Richard Cobden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> The Society for Promoting Women as County Councillors (SPWCC), a new women's rights group, had proposed Cobden as the candidate for Bow and Bromley and <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Sandhurst" class="mw-redirect" title="Margaret Sandhurst">Margaret Sandhurst</a> for Brixton.<sup id="cite_ref-S63_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S63-24">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[n 3]</a></sup> Lansbury counselled Cobden in the issues of greatest concern to the East End electorate: housing for the poor, ending of <a href="/wiki/Sweatshop" title="Sweatshop">sweated labour</a>, rights of public assembly, and control of the police. Specific questions of women's rights were largely avoided during the campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup> On 19 January 1889 both women were elected; these triumphs were, however, short-lived. Sandhurst's qualification to serve as a county councillor was successfully challenged in the courts by her <a href="/wiki/Conservative_party_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> opponents on the grounds of her sex, and her subsequent appeal was dismissed. Cobden was not immediately challenged, but in April 1891, after a series of legal actions, she was effectively neutered as a councillor by being prevented from voting on pain of severe financial penalties.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup> Lansbury urged her, during the hearings, to "go to prison and let the Council back you up by refusing to declare your seat vacant".<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup> Cobden did not follow this path. A Bill introduced in the House of Commons in May 1891 permitting women to serve as county councillors found little support among MPs of any party; women were not granted this right until 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Lansbury was offended by his party's lukewarm support for women's rights. In a letter published in the <i><a href="/wiki/Pall_Mall_Gazette" class="mw-redirect" title="Pall Mall Gazette">Pall Mall Gazette</a></i> he made an open call to Bow and Bromley's Liberals to "shake themselves free of party feeling and throw the energy and ability they are now wasting on minor questions into&nbsp;... securing the full rights of citizenship to every woman in the land".<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup> He was further disillusioned by his party's failure to endorse the eight-hour maximum working day. Lansbury had formed the view, expressed some years later, that "Liberalism would progress just as far as the great money bags of capitalism would allow it to progress".<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup> By 1892 the Liberals no longer felt like Lansbury's political home; most of his current associates were avowed socialists: <a href="/wiki/William_Morris" title="William Morris">William Morris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Marx" title="Eleanor Marx">Eleanor Marx</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Burns" title="John Burns">John Burns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Mayers_Hyndman" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Mayers Hyndman">Henry Mayers Hyndman</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Federation" title="Social Democratic Federation">Social Democratic Federation</a> (SDF).<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Lansbury did not resign from the Liberals until he had fulfilled a commitment to act as election agent for <a href="/wiki/John_Macdonald_(British_politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Macdonald (British politician)">John Murray MacDonald</a>, the prospective Liberal candidate for Bow and Bromley. He saw his candidate victorious in the <a href="/wiki/1892_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1892 United Kingdom general election">July 1892 General Election</a>; as soon as the result was declared, Lansbury resigned from the Liberal Party and joined the SDF.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Socialist_reformer">Socialist reformer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Socialist reformer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_Democratic_Federation">Social Democratic Federation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Social Democratic Federation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Hyndman_0001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Henry_Hyndman_0001.jpg/170px-Henry_Hyndman_0001.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Henry_Hyndman_0001.jpg/255px-Henry_Hyndman_0001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Henry_Hyndman_0001.jpg/340px-Henry_Hyndman_0001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2901" data-file-height="3856"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Hyndman_0001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Henry Hyndman, founder of the SDF, was a key influence in Lansbury's early career.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Lansbury's choice of the SDF, from several socialist organisations, reflected his admiration for Hyndman, whom he considered "one of the truly great ones".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup> Lansbury quickly became the federation's most tireless propagandist, travelling throughout Britain to address meetings or to demonstrate solidarity with workers involved in industrial disputes.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[n 4]</a></sup> Around this time, Lansbury temporarily set aside his Christian beliefs and became a member of the East London <a href="/wiki/Ethical_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethical Society">Ethical Society</a>. One factor in his disillusion with the Church was the local clergy's unsympathetic approach to poor relief, and their opposition to collective political action.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1895 Lansbury fought two parliamentary elections for the SDF in <a href="/wiki/Newington_Walworth_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" class="mw-redirect" title="Newington Walworth (UK Parliament constituency)">Walworth</a>, first a by-election on 14 May, then the <a href="/wiki/1895_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1895 United Kingdom general election">1895 general election</a> two months later. Despite his energetic campaigning he was heavily defeated on each occasion, with tiny proportions of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[34]</a></sup> After these dismal results, Lansbury was persuaded by Hyndman to give up his job at the sawmill and become the SDF's full-time salaried national organiser. He preached a straightforward revolutionary doctrine: "The time has arrived", he informed an audience at <a href="/wiki/Todmorden" title="Todmorden">Todmorden</a> in Lancashire, "for the working classes to seize political power and use it to overthrow the competitive system and establish in its place state cooperation".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup> Lansbury's time as SDF national organiser did not last long; in 1896, when Isaac Brine died suddenly, Lansbury thought that his family duty required him to take charge of the sawmill, and he returned home to Bow.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[36]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/1900_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1900 United Kingdom general election">general election of 1900</a> a pact with the Liberals in the Bow and Bromley constituency gave Lansbury, the SDF candidate, a straight fight against the Conservative incumbent, William Guthrie. Lansbury's cause was hindered by his public opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Boer_War#Second_Anglo-Boer_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Boer War">Boer War</a> at a time when war fever was strong, while Guthrie, a former soldier, stressed his military credentials. Lansbury lost the election, though his total of 2,258 votes against Guthrie's 4,403 was considered creditable by the press.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[37]</a></sup> This campaign was Lansbury's last major effort on behalf of the SDF. He became disenchanted by Hyndman's inability to work with other socialist groups, and in about 1903 resigned from the SDF to join the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Labour_Party" title="Independent Labour Party">Independent Labour Party</a> (ILP).<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[38]</a></sup> At around this time, Lansbury rediscovered his Christian faith and rejoined the Anglican Church.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[n 5]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poor_Law_guardian">Poor Law guardian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Poor Law guardian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:246px; ; background-color: #c6dbf7;">
       <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r887775652">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style> 
       <div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
        "'You scratch my back and I'll scratch yours' was the basis of policy where jobs and contracts were concerned&nbsp;... the slum owner and agent could be depended upon to create the conditions which produce disease; the doctor would then get the job of attending the sick, the chemist would be needed to supply drugs, the parson to pray, and when, between them all, the victims died the undertaker was on hand to bury them."
       </div> 
       <p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">(Lansbury, summarising the extent of cronyism and abuse in the Poor Law system.)<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[41]</a></sup></cite> </p> 
      </div> 
      <p>In April 1893 Lansbury achieved his first elective office when he became a <a href="/wiki/Board_of_guardians" title="Board of guardians">Poor Law guardian</a> for the district of <a href="/wiki/Poplar,_London" title="Poplar, London">Poplar</a>. In place of the traditionally harsh <a href="/wiki/Workhouse" title="Workhouse">workhouse</a> regime that was the norm, Lansbury proposed a programme of reform, whereby the workhouse became "an agency of help instead of a place of despair", and the stigma of poverty was removed.<sup id="cite_ref-S54_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S54-47">[42]</a></sup> Lansbury was one of a minority socialist bloc which was often able, through its energy and commitment, to win support for its plans.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[43]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Education for the poor was one of Lansbury's major concerns. He helped to transform the Forest Gate District School, previously a punitive establishment run on quasi-military lines, into a proper place of education that became the Poplar Training School, and was still in existence more than half a century later.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[n 6]</a></sup> At the 1897 annual Poor Law Conference Lansbury summarised his views on poor relief in his first published paper: "The Principles of the English Poor Law". His analysis offered a <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> critique of capitalism: only the reorganisation of industry on collectivist lines would solve contemporary problems.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Lansbury added to his public duties when, in 1903, he was elected to <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Borough_of_Poplar" title="Metropolitan Borough of Poplar">Poplar Borough Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[47]</a></sup> In the summer of that year he met <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Fels" title="Joseph Fels">Joseph Fels</a>, a rich American soap manufacturer with a penchant for social projects.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[48]</a></sup> Lansbury persuaded Fels, in 1904, to purchase a 100-acre farm at <a href="/wiki/Laindon" title="Laindon">Laindon</a>, in Essex, which was converted into a labour colony that provided regular work for Poplar's unemployed and destitute. Fels also agreed to finance a much larger colony at <a href="/wiki/Hollesley_Bay,_Suffolk" class="mw-redirect" title="Hollesley Bay, Suffolk">Hollesley Bay</a> in Suffolk, to be operated as a government scheme under the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Board" title="Local Government Board">Local Government Board</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[49]</a></sup> Both projects were initially successful, but were undermined after the election of a Liberal government in 1906. The new Local Government minister was John Burns, a former SDF stalwart now ensconced in the Liberal Party who had become a firm opponent of socialism.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[51]</a></sup> Burns encouraged a campaign of propaganda to discredit the principle of labour colonies, which were presented as money-wasting ventures that pampered idlers and scroungers. A formal enquiry revealed irregularities in the operation of the scheme, though it exonerated Lansbury. He retained the confidence of his electorate and was easily re-elected to the Board of Guardians in 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-P79_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P79-59">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1905 Lansbury was appointed to a Royal Commission on the Poor Laws, which deliberated for four years. Lansbury, together with <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_Webb" title="Beatrice Webb">Beatrice Webb</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Society" title="Fabian Society">Fabian Society</a>, argued for the complete abolition of the Poor Laws and their replacement by a system that incorporated old age pensions, a minimum wage, and national and local public works projects. These proposals were embodied at the Commission's conclusion in a minority report signed by Lansbury and Webb; the majority report was, according to Postgate, "an ill-considered jumble of suggestions&nbsp;... so preposterously inadequate that no attempts were ever made to implement it." Most of the minority's recommendations in time became national policy;<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[54]</a></sup> the Poor Laws were finally abolished by the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1929" title="Local Government Act 1929">Local Government Act 1929</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[55]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="National_prominence">National prominence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: National prominence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span id="Campaigner_for_women.27s_suffrage"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaigner_for_women's_suffrage">Campaigner for women's suffrage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Campaigner for women's suffrage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Cat_and_Mouse_Act_Poster_-_1914.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Cat_and_Mouse_Act_Poster_-_1914.jpg/170px-Cat_and_Mouse_Act_Poster_-_1914.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Cat_and_Mouse_Act_Poster_-_1914.jpg/255px-Cat_and_Mouse_Act_Poster_-_1914.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Cat_and_Mouse_Act_Poster_-_1914.jpg/340px-Cat_and_Mouse_Act_Poster_-_1914.jpg 2x" data-file-width="390" data-file-height="600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Cat_and_Mouse_Act_Poster_-_1914.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>WSPU poster from 1914, denouncing the Liberal government's controversial "
         <a href="/wiki/Cat_and_Mouse_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Cat and Mouse Act">Cat and Mouse Act</a>"
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/1906_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1906 United Kingdom general election">general election of January 1906</a> Lansbury stood as an independent socialist candidate in <a href="/wiki/Middlesbrough_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Middlesbrough (UK Parliament constituency)">Middlesbrough</a>, on a strong "votes for women" platform. This was his first campaign based on women's rights since the LCC election of 1889. He had been recommended to the constituency by Joseph Fels, who agreed to meet his expenses. The local ILP leadership was committed by an electoral pact to support the Liberal candidate, and could not endorse Lansbury, who secured less than 9 per cent of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-S83_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S83-62">[56]</a></sup> The campaign had been managed by <a href="/wiki/Marion_Coates_Hansen" title="Marion Coates Hansen">Marion Coates Hansen</a>, a prominent local suffragist. Under Hansen's influence Lansbury took up the cause of "votes for women";<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[57]</a></sup> he allied himself with the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Social_and_Political_Union" title="Women's Social and Political Union">Women's Social and Political Union</a> (WSPU), the more militant of the main suffragist organisations, and became a close associate of <a href="/wiki/Emmeline_Pankhurst" title="Emmeline Pankhurst">Emmeline Pankhurst</a> and her family.<sup id="cite_ref-Schneer_1990,_p._95_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schneer_1990,_p._95-64">[58]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Liberal government elected in 1906 with a large majority showed little interest in the issue of women's suffrage;<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[59]</a></sup> when they lost their parliamentary majority in the <a href="/wiki/January_1910_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="January 1910 United Kingdom general election">general election of January 1910</a> they were dependent on the votes of the 40-odd Labour members.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[n 7]</a></sup> To Lansbury's dismay, Labour did not use this leverage to promote votes for women, instead giving the government virtually unqualified support to keep the Conservatives out of power.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[62]</a></sup> Lansbury had failed to win election as Labour's candidate at Bow and Bromley in January 1910; however, the continuing political crisis which developed from <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>'s controversial 1909 "<a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People's Budget">People's Budget</a>" led to another <a href="/wiki/December_1910_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="December 1910 United Kingdom general election">general election in December 1910</a>. Lansbury again fought Bow and Bromley, and this time was successful.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[63]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Lansbury found little support in his fight for women's suffrage from his parliamentary Labour colleagues, whom he dismissed as "a weak, flabby lot".<sup id="cite_ref-Schneer_1990,_p._95_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schneer_1990,_p._95-64">[58]</a></sup> In parliament, he denounced the prime minister, <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a>, for the cruelties being inflicted on imprisoned suffragists: "You are beneath contempt&nbsp;... you ought to be driven from public life". He was temporarily suspended from the House for "disorderly conduct".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[64]</a></sup> In October 1912, aware of the unbridgeable gap between his own position and that of his Labour colleagues, Lansbury resigned his seat to fight a <a href="/wiki/1912_Bow_and_Bromley_by-election" title="1912 Bow and Bromley by-election">by-election in Bow and Bromley</a> on the specific issue of women's suffrage.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[65]</a></sup> The suffragettes sent <a href="/wiki/Grace_Roe" title="Grace Roe">Grace Roe</a> to help with campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[66]</a></sup> He lost to his Conservative opponent, who campaigned on the slogan "No Petticoat Government".<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[67]</a></sup> Commenting on the result, the Labour MP <a href="/wiki/Will_Thorne" title="Will Thorne">Will Thorne</a> opined that no constituency could ever be won on the single question of votes for women.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[68]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Out of parliament, on 26 April 1913 Lansbury addressed a WSPU rally at the <a href="/wiki/Albert_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Albert Hall">Albert Hall</a>, and openly defended violent methods: "Let them burn and destroy property and do anything they will, and for every leader that is taken away, let a dozen step forward in their place". For this, Lansbury was charged with incitement, convicted and, after the dismissal of an appeal, sentenced to three months' imprisonment.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[69]</a></sup> He immediately went on hunger strike, and was released after four days; although liable to rearrest under the so-called <a href="/wiki/Prisoners_(Temporary_Discharge_for_Ill_Health)_Act_1913" title="Prisoners (Temporary Discharge for Ill Health) Act 1913">"Cat and Mouse Act"</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[n 8]</a></sup> he was thereafter left at liberty.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[71]</a></sup> In the autumn of 1913, at the invitation of Fels, Lansbury and his wife travelled to America and Canada for an extended holiday. On his return, he devoted his main efforts to the recently founded newspaper, the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Herald_(UK_newspaper)" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Herald (UK newspaper)">Daily Herald</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[72]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="War.2C_Daily_Herald_and_Bolshevism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="War,_Daily_Herald_and_Bolshevism">War, <i>Daily Herald</i> and Bolshevism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: War, Daily Herald and Bolshevism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: 
       <a href="/wiki/Daily_Herald_(UK_newspaper)" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Herald (UK newspaper)">Daily Herald (UK newspaper)</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:210px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:George_Lansbury.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3d/George_Lansbury.jpg" decoding="async" width="208" height="281" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="208" data-file-height="281"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:George_Lansbury.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Lansbury in 1920
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <i>Daily Herald</i> began as a temporary bulletin during the London printers' strike of 1910–11. After the strike ended, Lansbury and others raised sufficient funds for the <i>Herald</i> to be relaunched in April 1912 as a socialist daily newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[73]</a></sup> The paper attracted contributions from distinguished writers such as <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hilaire_Belloc" title="Hilaire Belloc">Hilaire Belloc</a>, <a href="/wiki/G._K._Chesterton" title="G. K. Chesterton">G. K. Chesterton</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a>, some of whom, Blythe notes, "weren't socialists at all but simply used [the paper] as a platform for their personal literary anarchy."<sup id="cite_ref-Blythe276_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blythe276-82">[74]</a></sup> Lansbury contributed regularly in support of his various causes, in particular the militant suffrage campaign,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[75]</a></sup> and early in 1914 assumed the paper's editorship.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[76]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:George_Lansbury_-_cartoon_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17653.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/George_Lansbury_-_cartoon_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17653.png/170px-George_Lansbury_-_cartoon_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17653.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/George_Lansbury_-_cartoon_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17653.png/255px-George_Lansbury_-_cartoon_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17653.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/George_Lansbury_-_cartoon_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17653.png/340px-George_Lansbury_-_cartoon_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17653.png 2x" data-file-width="2030" data-file-height="2530"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:George_Lansbury_-_cartoon_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17653.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A 
         <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i> cartoon of 22 September 1920, mocking Lansbury's denials of Bolshevist funding for the 
         <i>Daily Herald</i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Before the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a> in August 1914, the <i>Herald</i> took a strong anti-war line.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[77]</a></sup> Addressing a large demonstration in <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a> on 2 August 1914, Lansbury blamed the coming conflict on capitalism: "The workers of all countries have no quarrel. They are&nbsp;... exploited in times of peace and sent out to be massacred in times of war".<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[78]</a></sup> Lansbury's position was at odds with that of most of the Labour movement, which allied itself with the wartime coalition governments of Asquith and, from 1916, Lloyd George.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[n 9]</a></sup> In the prevailing jingoistic mood, numerous readers looked to the <i>Herald</i>—reduced by wartime economies to a weekly format—to present a balanced news perspective, untainted by war fever and chauvinism.<sup id="cite_ref-Blythe276_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blythe276-82">[74]</a></sup> During the winter of 1914–15, Lansbury visited the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a> trenches. He sent eye-witness accounts to the paper, which supported calls for a negotiated peace with Germany in line with President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>'s later "peace note" of January 1917. The paper also gave sympathetic coverage to conscientious objectors, and to Irish and Indian nationalists.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[80]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Lansbury used the pages of the <i>Daily Herald</i> to welcome the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution#February_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">February 1917 revolution in Russia</a> as "a new star of hope&nbsp;... arisen over Europe".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[81]</a></sup> At an Albert Hall rally on 18 March 1918 he hailed the spirit and enthusiasm of "this Russian movement", and urged his audience to "be ready to die, if necessary, for our faith".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[82]</a></sup> When the war ended in November 1918, Lloyd George called an <a href="/wiki/1918_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1918 United Kingdom general election">immediate general election</a>, correctly calculating that victory euphoria would keep his coalition in power. In this triumphalist climate, candidates such as Lansbury who had opposed the war found themselves unpopular, and he failed to retake his Bow and Bromley seat.<sup id="cite_ref-P183_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P183-92">[83]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <i>Herald</i> re-emerged as a daily paper in March 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[84]</a></sup> Under Lansbury's direction it maintained a strong and ultimately successful campaign against British intervention in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Civil_War" title="Russian Civil War">Russian Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Blythe276_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blythe276-82">[74]</a></sup> In February 1920 Lansbury travelled to Russia where he met <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Lenin</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[85]</a></sup> He published an account: <i>What I Saw in Russia</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-ODNB_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-5">[4]</a></sup> but the impact of the visit was overshadowed by accusations that the <i>Herald</i> was being financed from Bolshevist sources, a charge vehemently denied by Lansbury: "We have received no Bolshevist money, no Bolshevist paper, no Bolshevist bonds". Unknown to Lansbury, the allegations had some truth which, when exposed, caused him and the paper considerable embarrassment.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[86]</a></sup> By 1922 the <i>Herald</i><span style="padding-left:0.15em;">'</span>s financial problems had become such that it could no longer continue as a private venture financed by donations. Lansbury resigned the editorship and made the paper over to the Labour Party and the <a href="/wiki/Trades_Union_Congress" title="Trades Union Congress">Trades Union Congress</a> (TUC), although he continued to write for it and remained its titular general manager until 3 January 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[88]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id=".22Poplarism.22:_the_1921_rates_revolt"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Poplarism&quot;:_the_1921_rates_revolt">"Poplarism": the 1921 rates revolt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: &quot;Poplarism&quot;: the 1921 rates revolt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: 
       <a href="/wiki/Poplar_Rates_Rebellion" title="Poplar Rates Rebellion">Poplar Rates Rebellion</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mural_Poplar_Rates.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Mural_Poplar_Rates.jpg/220px-Mural_Poplar_Rates.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Mural_Poplar_Rates.jpg/330px-Mural_Poplar_Rates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Mural_Poplar_Rates.jpg/440px-Mural_Poplar_Rates.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mural_Poplar_Rates.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The 
         <a href="/wiki/Poplar_Rates_Rebellion_Mural" title="Poplar Rates Rebellion Mural">Poplar Rates Rebellion Mural</a> in Poplar commemorates the 1921 rates revolt.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Throughout his national campaigns, Lansbury remained a Poplar borough councillor and Poor Law guardian, and between 1910 and 1913 served a three-year term as a London County Councillor.<sup id="cite_ref-ODNB_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-5">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[89]</a></sup> In 1919 he became the first Labour mayor of <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Borough_of_Poplar" title="Metropolitan Borough of Poplar">Poplar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[90]</a></sup> Under the then-existing financial system for local government, boroughs were individually responsible for poor relief within their boundaries. This discriminated heavily against poorer councils such as Poplar, where <a href="/wiki/Rates_(tax)" title="Rates (tax)">rates</a> revenues were low and poverty and unemployment, always severe, were exacerbated in times of economic recession.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[n 10]</a></sup> Under this system, Postgate argues, "The wealthy West End boroughs were evading responsibility, as though the desolate and silent docks were the results of a failure by the Poplar Borough Council".<sup id="cite_ref-P216_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P216-100">[91]</a></sup> In addition to meeting the costs of its own obligations, the council was required to levy <a href="/wiki/Precept#Secular_law" title="Precept">precepts</a> to pay for services provided by bodies such as the London County Council and the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan Police">Metropolitan Police</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-S194_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S194-102">[92]</a></sup> Lansbury had long argued that a degree of rates equalisation across London was necessary, to share costs more fairly.<sup id="cite_ref-S54_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S54-47">[42]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>At its meeting on 22 March 1921 the Poplar Council resolved not to make its precepts and to apply these revenues to the costs of local poor relief.<sup id="cite_ref-S194_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S194-102">[92]</a></sup> This illegal action created a sensation, and led to legal proceedings against the council. On 29 July the thirty councillors involved marched in procession from Bow to the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice" title="High Court of Justice">High Court</a>, headed by a brass band. Informed by the judge that they must apply the precepts, the councillors would not budge; early in September, Lansbury and 29 fellow-councillors were imprisoned for contempt of court. Among those sentenced were his son <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Lansbury_(politician)" title="Edgar Lansbury (politician)">Edgar</a> and Edgar's wife, <a href="/wiki/Minnie_Lansbury" title="Minnie Lansbury">Minnie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-P216_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P216-100">[91]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The defiance of the Poplar councillors generated widespread interest and sympathy, and the publicity embarrassed the government. Several other Labour-controlled councils (including Stepney whose mayor was the future Labour leader <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a>) threatened similar policies.<sup id="cite_ref-S201_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S201-103">[93]</a></sup> After six weeks' incarceration the councillors were released, and a government conference was convened to resolve the matter. This conference brought a significant personal victory for Lansbury: the passage of the Local Authorities (Financial Provisions) Act, which equalised the poor relief burden across all the London boroughs. As a result, the rates in Poplar fell by a third, and additional revenues of £400,000 was gained by the borough.<sup id="cite_ref-P216_100-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P216-100">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-S201_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S201-103">[93]</a></sup> Lansbury was hailed as a hero; in the <a href="/wiki/1922_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1922 United Kingdom general election">1922 general election</a> he won the parliamentary seat of Bow and Bromley with a majority of nearly 7,000, and would hold it for the rest of his life. The term "Poplarism", always identified closely with Lansbury, became part of the political lexicon, applied generally to campaigns where local government stood against central government on behalf of the poor and least privileged of society.<sup id="cite_ref-ODNB_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-5">[4]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliament_and_national_office">Parliament and national office</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Parliament and national office">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour_backbencher">Labour backbencher</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Labour backbencher">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:246px; ; background-color: #c6dbf7;">
       <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"> 
       <div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
        "A few centuries ago one King who stood up against the common people of that day lost his head—really lost it&nbsp;... Since that day kings and queens had been what they ought to be if you had them. They never interfered with ordinary politics and George V would be well advised to keep his finger out of the pie now."
       </div> 
       <p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">(Lansbury's warning to the King shortly before the first Labour government took office in January 1924)<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[94]</a></sup></cite> </p> 
      </div> 
      <p>In May 1923 the Conservative prime minister, <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a>, resigned for health reasons. In December his successor, <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a>, called <a href="/wiki/1923_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1923 United Kingdom general election">another election</a> in which the Conservatives lost their majority, with Labour in a strong second place. <a href="/wiki/George_V" title="George V">King George V</a> advised Baldwin, as leader of the largest party, not to resign his office until defeated by a vote in the House of Commons. Defeat duly occurred on 21 January 1924, when the Liberals decided to throw in their lot with Labour. The king then asked Labour's leader, <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a>, to form a government.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Blythe278_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blythe278-106">[96]</a></sup> Lansbury caused royal offence by publicly implying that the king had colluded with other parties to keep Labour out, and by his references to the fate of <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[97]</a></sup> Despite his seniority, Lansbury was offered only a junior non-cabinet post in the new government, which he declined.<sup id="cite_ref-P225_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P225-108">[98]</a></sup> He believed that his exclusion from the cabinet followed pressure from the king.<sup id="cite_ref-P225_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P225-108">[98]</a></sup> At the 1923 Labour Party conference, while declaring himself a <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Republicanism in the United Kingdom">republican</a>, Lansbury opposed two motions calling for the abolition of the monarchy, deeming the issue a "distraction". Social revolution, he said, would one day remove the monarchy.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[99]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:217px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:George_Lansbury_at_Skara_Brae,_1929.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b9/George_Lansbury_at_Skara_Brae%2C_1929.jpg" decoding="async" width="215" height="286" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="215" data-file-height="286"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:George_Lansbury_at_Skara_Brae,_1929.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Inspecting the Neolithic village of 
         <a href="/wiki/Skara_Brae" title="Skara Brae">Skara Brae</a>, 
         <a href="/wiki/Orkneys" class="mw-redirect" title="Orkneys">Orkneys</a>, when First Commissioner at the Office of Works in 1929
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>MacDonald's administration lasted less than a year before, in November 1924, the Liberals withdrew their support; Blythe comments that the first Labour government had been "neither exhilarating nor competent".<sup id="cite_ref-Blythe278_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blythe278-106">[96]</a></sup> According to Shepherd, MacDonald's chief priority was to show that Labour was "fit to govern", and he had thus acted with conservative caution.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[100]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1924_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1924 United Kingdom general election">December general election</a> returned the Conservatives to power; Lansbury maintained that Labour's cause "marches forward irrespective of electoral results".<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[101]</a></sup> After the defeat Lansbury was briefly touted as an alternative party leader to MacDonald, a proposition he rejected.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[102]</a></sup> In 1925, free from the <i>Daily Herald</i>, he founded and edited <i>Lansbury's Labour Weekly</i>, which became a mouthpiece for his personal creed of socialism, democracy and pacifism until it merged with the <i><a href="/wiki/Labour_Leader" title="Labour Leader">New Leader</a></i> in 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[103]</a></sup> Before the <a href="/wiki/1926_United_Kingdom_general_strike" title="1926 United Kingdom general strike">General Strike</a> of May 1926, Lansbury used the <i>Weekly</i> to instruct the <a href="/wiki/Trades_Union_Congress" title="Trades Union Congress">Trades Union Congress</a> (TUC) on preparations for the coming struggle. However, when the strike came the TUC did not want his assistance;<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[104]</a></sup> among the reasons for their distrust was Lansbury's continuing advocacy for the right of communist organisations to affiliate to the Labour Party—he privately opined that British communists on their own "couldn't run a whelk-stall".<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[105]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Lansbury continued his private campaigns in parliament, saying "I intend on every occasion to&nbsp;... hinder the progress of business".<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[106]</a></sup> In April 1926 he and 12 other opposition MPs prevented a vote in the House of Commons by obstructing the voting lobbies; they were temporarily suspended by the <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_House_of_Commons_(United_Kingdom)" title="Speaker of the House of Commons (United Kingdom)">Speaker</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[108]</a></sup> During frequent clashes in the House with <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Health" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Health">Minister of Health</a> responsible for Poor Law administration and reform, Lansbury referred to the "Ministry of Death",<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[109]</a></sup> and called the minister a "pinchbeck Napoleon".<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[110]</a></sup> However, within the Labour Party itself, Lansbury's status and popularity led to his election as the party's chairman (a largely titular office) in 1927–28.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[111]</a></sup> Lansbury also became president of the International League Against Imperialism, where among his fellow executive members were <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soong_Ching-ling" title="Soong Ching-ling">Mme. Sun Yat-sen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[112]</a></sup> In 1928, short of money following the failure of the family business, Lansbury published his autobiography, <i>My Life</i>, for which he received what he termed "a fairly generous cheque" from the publishers, <a href="/wiki/Constable_%26_Robinson" title="Constable &amp; Robinson">Constable &amp; Co.</a><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[113]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Cabinet_minister.2C_1929.E2.80.9331"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cabinet_minister,_1929–31">Cabinet minister, 1929–31</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cabinet minister, 1929–31">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Hyde_Park_(6853353480).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Hyde_Park_%286853353480%29.jpg/220px-Hyde_Park_%286853353480%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Hyde_Park_%286853353480%29.jpg/330px-Hyde_Park_%286853353480%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Hyde_Park_%286853353480%29.jpg/440px-Hyde_Park_%286853353480%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1045" data-file-height="617"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Hyde_Park_(6853353480).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Hyde Park Lido, one lasting result of Lansbury's brief term of national office
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/1929_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1929 United Kingdom general election">1929 general election</a> Labour emerged as the largest party, with 287 seats—but without an overall majority.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[114]</a></sup> Once again, MacDonald formed a government dependent on Liberal support. Lansbury joined the new cabinet as <a href="/wiki/First_Commissioner_of_Works" title="First Commissioner of Works">First Commissioner of Works</a>, with responsibilities for historic buildings and monuments and for the royal parks. This position was widely regarded as a <a href="/wiki/Sinecure" title="Sinecure">sinecure</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[115]</a></sup> nevertheless, Lansbury proved an active minister who did much to improve public recreation facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-ODNB_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-5">[4]</a></sup> His most notable achievement was the Lido on the <a href="/wiki/Serpentine_(lake)" class="mw-redirect" title="Serpentine (lake)">Serpentine</a> in Hyde Park; according to the historian <a href="/wiki/A._J._P._Taylor" title="A. J. P. Taylor">A. J. P. Taylor</a> "the only thing which keeps the memory of the second Labour government alive".<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[116]</a></sup> A circular memorial plaque to Lansbury can be seen on the exterior wall of the Lido Bar and Cafe. Lansbury's duties brought him into frequent contact with the King, who as Ranger of the royal parks insisted on regular consultation. Contrary to the expectations of some the two formed a cordial relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[118]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The years of MacDonald's second government were dominated by the economic depression that followed the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Crash" class="mw-redirect" title="Wall Street Crash">Wall Street Crash</a> of October 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[119]</a></sup> Lansbury was appointed to a committee, chaired by <a href="/wiki/J.H._Thomas" class="mw-redirect" title="J.H. Thomas">J.H. Thomas</a> and including Sir <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Mosley" title="Oswald Mosley">Oswald Mosley</a>, charged with finding a solution to unemployment. Mosley produced a memorandum which called for a large-scale programme of public works; this was rejected by the <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Snowden,_1st_Viscount_Snowden" title="Philip Snowden, 1st Viscount Snowden">Philip Snowden</a>, on grounds of cost.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-T404_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T404-131">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[n 11]</a></sup> At the end of July 1931 the <a href="/wiki/May_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="May Committee">May Committee</a>, appointed in February to investigate government spending,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[123]</a></sup> prescribed heavy cuts, including a massive reduction in unemployment benefit.<sup id="cite_ref-T362_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T362-135">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[n 12]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:George_Lansbury,_c1935.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/George_Lansbury%2C_c1935.jpg/220px-George_Lansbury%2C_c1935.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/George_Lansbury%2C_c1935.jpg/330px-George_Lansbury%2C_c1935.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/George_Lansbury%2C_c1935.jpg/440px-George_Lansbury%2C_c1935.jpg 2x" data-file-width="502" data-file-height="382"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:George_Lansbury,_c1935.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Making a speech, c. 1935
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>During August, in an atmosphere of financial panic and a run on the pound, the government debated the report. MacDonald and Snowden were prepared to implement it, but Lansbury and nine other cabinet ministers rejected the cut in unemployment benefit. Thus divided, the government could not continue; MacDonald, however, did not resign as prime minister. After discussions with the opposition leaders and the king he formed a national all-party coalition, with a "doctor's mandate" to tackle the economic crisis. The great majority of Labour MPs, including Lansbury, were opposed to this action; MacDonald and the few who followed him were expelled from the party, and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Henderson" title="Arthur Henderson">Arthur Henderson</a> became leader.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[125]</a></sup> MacDonald's move was broadly welcomed in the country, however, and in the <a href="/wiki/1931_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1931 United Kingdom general election">general election held in October 1931</a> the national government was returned with an enormous majority. Labour was reduced to 46 members; Lansbury was the only senior member of the Labour leadership to retain his seat.<sup id="cite_ref-T404_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T404-131">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[n 13]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Party_leader">Party leader</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Party leader">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Although defeated in the election, Henderson remained the party leader while Lansbury headed the Labour group in parliament—the <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Labour_Party" title="Parliamentary Labour Party">Parliamentary Labour Party</a> (PLP). In October 1932 Henderson resigned and Lansbury succeeded him.<sup id="cite_ref-S282_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S282-1">[1]</a></sup> In most historians' reckonings, Lansbury led his small parliamentary force with skill and flair. He was also, says Shepherd, an inspiration to the dispirited Labour rank and file.<sup id="cite_ref-S286_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S286-140">[127]</a></sup> As leader he began the process of reforming the party's organisation and machinery, efforts which resulted in considerable by-election and municipal election successes—including control of the LCC under <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Morrison" title="Herbert Morrison">Herbert Morrison</a> in 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-ODNB_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-5">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[128]</a></sup> According to Blythe, Lansbury "represented political hope and decency to the three million unemployed."<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[129]</a></sup> During this period Lansbury published his political credo, <i>My England</i> (1934), which envisioned a future socialist state achieved by a mixture of revolutionary and evolutionary methods.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[130]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:246px; ; background-color: #c6dbf7;">
       <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"> 
       <div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
