        I believe that force never has and never will bring permanent peace and goodwill in the world&nbsp;... God intends us to live peacefully and quietly with one another. If some people do not allow us to do so, I am ready to stand as the early Christians did, and say, this is our faith, this is where we stand, and, if necessary, this is where we will die.
       </div> 
       <p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">(From Lansbury's speech to the 1935 Labour Party conference)<sup id="cite_ref-V115_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-V115-144">[131]</a></sup></cite> </p> 
      </div> 
      <p>The small Labour group in parliament had little influence over economic policy; Lansbury's term as leader was dominated by foreign affairs and disarmament, and by policy disagreements within the Labour movement. The official party position was based on collective security through the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> and on multilateral disarmament. Lansbury, supported by many in the PLP, adopted a position of Christian pacifism, unilateral disarmament and the dismantling of the British Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-V107_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-V107-145">[132]</a></sup> Under his influence the party's 1933 conference passed resolutions calling for the "total disarmament of all nations", and pledged to take no part in war.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[133]</a></sup> Pacifism became temporarily popular in the country; on 9 February 1933 the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Union" title="Oxford Union">Oxford Union</a> <a href="/wiki/The_King_and_Country_debate" title="The King and Country debate">voted</a> by 275 to 153 that it would "in no circumstances fight for its King and Country", and the <a href="/wiki/1933_Fulham_East_by-election" title="1933 Fulham East by-election">Fulham East by-election</a> in October 1933 was easily won by a Labour candidate committed to full disarmament.<sup id="cite_ref-B285_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B285-147">[134]</a></sup> Lansbury sent a message to the constituency in his position as Labour Leader: "I would close every recruiting station, disband the Army and disarm the Air Force. I would abolish the whole dreadful equipment of war and say to the world: “Do your worst”."<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[135]</a></sup> October 1934 saw the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Peace_Pledge_Union" title="Peace Pledge Union">Peace Pledge Union</a>. In response to the Peace Pledge Union, the League of Nations Union conducted the 1934–35 <a href="/wiki/Peace_Ballot" title="Peace Ballot">Peace Ballot</a>, an unofficial public referendum, which produced massive majorities in support of the League of Nations, multilateral disarmament, and conflict resolution through non-military means - though crucially, a three-fold majority supported military measures as a last resort. Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> had come to power in Germany, and had withdrawn from the international Conference on Disarmament in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>. Blythe observes that Britain's noisy flirtations with pacifism "drowned out the sounds from German dockyards", as German rearmament began.<sup id="cite_ref-B285_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B285-147">[134]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As fascism and militarism advanced in Europe, Lansbury's pacifist stance drew criticism from the trades union elements of his party—who controlled the majority of party conference votes. <a href="/wiki/Walter_Citrine" class="mw-redirect" title="Walter Citrine">Walter Citrine</a>, the <a href="/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Trades_Union_Congress" title="General Secretary of the Trades Union Congress">TUC general secretary</a>, commented that Lansbury "thinks the country should be without defence of any kind&nbsp;... it certainly isn't our policy."<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[136]</a></sup> The party's 1935 annual conference took place in Brighton during October, under the shadow of Italy's impending invasion of <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Abyssinia</a>. The national executive had tabled a resolution calling for sanctions against Italy, which Lansbury opposed as a form of economic warfare. His speech—a passionate exposition of the principles of Christian pacifism—was well received by the delegates, but immediately afterwards his position was destroyed by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Bevin" title="Ernest Bevin">Ernest Bevin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Transport_and_General_Workers%27_Union" title="Transport and General Workers' Union">Transport and General Workers' Union</a> leader. Bevin attacked Lansbury for putting his private beliefs before a policy, agreed by all the party's main institutions, to oppose fascist aggression,<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[137]</a></sup> and accused him of "hawking your conscience round from body to body asking to be told what to do with it".<sup id="cite_ref-S323_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S323-151">[138]</a></sup> Union support ensured that the sanctions resolution was carried by a huge majority; Lansbury, realising that a Christian pacifist could no longer lead the party, resigned a few days later.<sup id="cite_ref-V115_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-V115-144">[131]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years">Final years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Final years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:246px; ; background-color: #c6dbf7;">
       <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"> 
       <div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
        [Hitler] appeared free of personal ambition&nbsp;... wasn't ashamed of his humble start in life&nbsp;... lived in the country rather than the town&nbsp;... was a bachelor who liked children and old people&nbsp;... and was obviously lonely. I wished that I could have gone to Berchtesgaden and stayed with him for a little while. I felt that Christianity in its purest sense might have had a chance with him.
       </div> 
       <p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">(Lansbury's impressions after meeting Adolf Hitler in April 1937)<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[139]</a></sup></cite> </p> 
      </div> 
      <p>Lansbury was 76 years old when he resigned the Labour leadership; he did not, however, retire from public life. In the <a href="/wiki/1935_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1935 United Kingdom general election">general election of November 1935</a> he kept his seat at Bow and Bromley; Labour, now led by Attlee, improved its parliamentary representation to 154.<sup id="cite_ref-S323_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S323-151">[138]</a></sup> Lansbury devoted himself entirely to the cause of world peace, a quest that took him, in 1936, to the United States. He addressed large crowds in 27 cities before meeting <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">President Roosevelt</a> in Washington to present his proposals for a world peace conference.<sup id="cite_ref-Schneer180_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schneer180-153">[140]</a></sup> In 1937 he toured Europe, visiting leaders in Belgium, France and Scandinavia, and in April secured a private meeting with Hitler. No official report of the discussion was issued, but Lansbury's personal memorandum indicates that Hitler expressed willingness to join in a world conference if Roosevelt would convene it.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[141]</a></sup> Later that year Lansbury met <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Mussolini</a> in Rome; he described the Italian leader as "a mixture of Lloyd George, Stanley Baldwin and Winston Churchill".<sup id="cite_ref-S341_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S341-155">[142]</a></sup> Lansbury wrote several accounts of his peace journeys, notably <i>My Quest for Peace</i> (1938).<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[143]</a></sup> His mild and optimistic impressions of the European dictators were widely criticised as naïve and out of touch; some British pacifists were dismayed at Lansbury's meeting with Hitler,<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[144]</a></sup> while the <i><a href="/wiki/Morning_Star_(UK_newspaper)" class="mw-redirect" title="Morning Star (UK newspaper)">Daily Worker</a></i> accused him of diverting attention from the aggressive realities of fascist policies.<sup id="cite_ref-S341_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S341-155">[142]</a></sup> Lansbury continued to meet European leaders through 1938 and 1939, and was nominated, unsuccessfully, for the 1940 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-S343_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S343-158">[145]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:George_Lansbury,_1936.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/George_Lansbury%2C_1936.jpg/220px-George_Lansbury%2C_1936.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/George_Lansbury%2C_1936.jpg/330px-George_Lansbury%2C_1936.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/72/George_Lansbury%2C_1936.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="518"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:George_Lansbury,_1936.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>At the funeral of George V, 1936
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>At home, Lansbury served a second term as Mayor of Poplar, in 1936–37. He argued against direct confrontation with Mosley's <a href="/wiki/British_Union_of_Fascists" title="British Union of Fascists">Blackshirts</a> during the October 1936 demonstrations known as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cable_Street" title="Battle of Cable Street">Battle of Cable Street</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[146]</a></sup> In October 1937 he became president of the Peace Pledge Union,<sup id="cite_ref-Schneer180_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schneer180-153">[140]</a></sup> and a year later he welcomed the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">Munich Agreement</a> as a step towards peace. During this period he worked on behalf of refugees from Nazi Germany, and was chairman of the Polish Refugee Fund which provided relief to displaced Jewish children.<sup id="cite_ref-S343_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S343-158">[145]</a></sup> On 3 September 1939, after <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a>'s announcement of war with Germany, Lansbury addressed the House of Commons. Observing that the cause to which he had dedicated his life was going down in ruins, he added: "I hope that out of this terrible calamity will arise a spirit that will compel people to give up the reliance on force."<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[147]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Early in 1940 Lansbury's health began to fail; although unaware, he was suffering from <a href="/wiki/Stomach_cancer" title="Stomach cancer">stomach cancer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-S343_158-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S343-158">[145]</a></sup> In an article published in the socialist magazine <i>Tribune</i>, published on 25 April 1940, he made a final statement of his Christian pacifism: "I hold fast to the truth that this world is big enough for all, that we are all brethren, children of one Father".<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[148]</a></sup> Lansbury died on 7 May 1940, at the Manor House Hospital in <a href="/wiki/Golders_Green" title="Golders Green">Golders Green</a>. His funeral in <a href="/wiki/St_Mary%27s_Church,_Bow" class="mw-redirect" title="St Mary's Church, Bow">St Mary's Church, Bow</a>, was followed by cremation at Ilford Crematorium,<sup id="cite_ref-Holman164_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holman164-162">[149]</a></sup> before a memorial service in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ODNB_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-5">[4]</a></sup> His ashes were scattered at sea, in accordance with the wish expressed in his will: "I desire this because although I love England very dearly&nbsp;... I am a convinced internationalist".<sup id="cite_ref-Holman164_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holman164-162">[149]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tributes_and_legacy">Tributes and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Tributes and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Most historical assessments of Lansbury have tended to stress his character and principles rather than his effectiveness as a party political leader. His biographer Jonathan Schneer writes: </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>Lansbury was a talented politician, speaker, and organizer. What made him remarkable was the stubbornness with which he clung to his principles... [He] became one of the best-loved and most-respected figures in the labour movement. Lansbury's legacy has been the adamantine insistence among an element within the Labour Party that Britain must stand for moral principles, must set the world a moral example. Concretely, this has meant demanding the total abolition of capitalism and unilateral disarmament, policies that Labour's leaders have usually thought utopian or worse.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[150]</a></sup> </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Urban_blooms2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Urban_blooms2006.jpg/220px-Urban_blooms2006.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Urban_blooms2006.jpg/330px-Urban_blooms2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Urban_blooms2006.jpg/440px-Urban_blooms2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Urban_blooms2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Lansbury Estate, Poplar
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Historian <a href="/wiki/A._J._P._Taylor" title="A. J. P. Taylor">A. J. P. Taylor</a> labeled Lansbury as "the most lovable figure in modern politics" and the outstanding figure of the English revolutionary left in the 20th century,<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[151]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_O._Morgan" title="Kenneth O. Morgan">Kenneth O. Morgan</a>, in his biography of a later Labour leader, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot</a>, regards Lansbury as "an agitator of protest, not a politician of power".<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[152]</a></sup> Journalists commonly accused Lansbury of sentimentality, and party intellectuals accused him of lacking mental capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-S360_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S360-166">[153]</a></sup> Nevertheless, his speeches in the House of Commons were often flavoured with historical and literary allusions, and he left behind a considerable body of writing on socialist ideas; Morgan refers to him as a "prophet".<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[154]</a></sup> Foot, who as a young man met and was influenced by Lansbury, was particularly impressed by the older man's achievements in establishing the <i>Daily Herald</i>, given his complete lack of journalistic training.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[155]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Foot felt that Lansbury's pacifism was unrealistic, and believed that Bevin's demolition at the 1935 conference was justified.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[156]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There is much agreement among historians and analysts that Lansbury was never self-serving and, guided by his <a href="/wiki/Christian_socialism" title="Christian socialism">Christian socialist</a> principles, was consistent in his efforts on behalf of the poorest in society.<sup id="cite_ref-ODNB_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-5">[4]</a></sup> Shepherd believes that "there could have been no better leader for the Labour Party at the collapse of its political fortunes in 1931 than Lansbury, a universally popular choice and a source of inspiration among Labour ranks".<sup id="cite_ref-S286_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S286-140">[127]</a></sup> In the House of Commons on 8 May 1940, the day following Lansbury's death, Chamberlain said of him: "There were not many hon. Members who felt convinced of the practicability of the methods which he advocated for the preservation of peace, but there was no one who did not realise his intense conviction, which arose out of his deep humanitarianism". Attlee also paid tribute to his former leader: "He hated cruelty, injustice and wrongs, and felt deeply for all who suffered&nbsp;... [H]e was ever the champion of the weak, and&nbsp;... to the end of his life he strove for that in which he believed".<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[157]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After the Second World War, a stained glass window designed by the Belgian artist Eugeen Yoors was placed in the <a href="/wiki/Kingsley_Hall" title="Kingsley Hall">Kingsley Hall</a> community centre in Bow, as a memorial to Lansbury. His memory is further sustained by streets and housing developments named after him, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Lansbury_Estate" title="Lansbury Estate">Lansbury Estate</a> in Poplar, completed in 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-S360_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S360-166">[153]</a></sup> A further enduring memorial, Attlee suggests, is the extent to which the then-revolutionary social policies that Lansbury began advocating before the turn of the 20th century had become accepted mainstream doctrine little more than a decade after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[158]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>His name and picture (and those of 58 other women's suffrage supporters) are on the <a href="/wiki/Plinth" class="mw-redirect" title="Plinth">plinth</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Millicent_Fawcett" title="Statue of Millicent Fawcett">statue of Millicent Fawcett</a> in <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Square" title="Parliament Square">Parliament Square</a>, London, unveiled in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-gov_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gov-172">[159]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[161]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_and_family_life">Personal and family life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Personal and family life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>George married Elizabeth Jane (Bessie) Brine on 22 May 1880 in Whitechapel, London. For most of their married life, George and Bessie Lansbury lived in Bow, originally in St Stephen's Road and, from 1916, at 39 Bow Road, a house which, Shepherd records, became "a political haven" for those requiring assistance of any kind.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[162]</a></sup> Bessie died in 1933, after 53 years of a marriage that had produced 12 children between 1881 and 1905.<sup id="cite_ref-S347_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S347-176">[163]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Of the ten who survived to adulthood, Edgar followed his father into local political activism as a Poplar councillor in 1912, serving as the borough's mayor in 1924–25. He was for a time a member of the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Great_Britain" title="Communist Party of Great Britain">Communist Party of Great Britain</a> (CPGB). After the death of his first wife Minnie in 1922, Edgar married <a href="/wiki/Moyna_Macgill" title="Moyna Macgill">Moyna Macgill</a>, an actress from Belfast;<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[164]</a></sup> their daughter Dame <a href="/wiki/Angela_Lansbury" title="Angela Lansbury">Angela Lansbury</a>, born in 1925, became a stage and screen actress.<sup id="cite_ref-S347_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S347-176">[163]</a></sup> George Lansbury's youngest daughter Violet (1900–71) was an active CPGB member in the 1920s, who lived and worked in Moscow for many years. She married Clemens Palme Dutt, the brother of the Marxist intellectual <a href="/wiki/Rajani_Palme_Dutt" class="mw-redirect" title="Rajani Palme Dutt">Rajani Palme Dutt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[165]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Another daughter, <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Lansbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Dorothy Lansbury">Dorothy</a> (1890–1973), was a women's rights activist and later a campaigner for contraceptive and abortion rights. She married <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Thurtle" title="Ernest Thurtle">Ernest Thurtle</a>, the Labour MP for <a href="/wiki/Shoreditch_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Shoreditch (UK Parliament constituency)">Shoreditch</a>, and was herself a member of Shoreditch council, serving as mayor in 1936. She and her husband founded the Workers' Birth Control Group in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[166]</a></sup> Her younger sister Daisy (1892–1971) served as George Lansbury's secretary for 20 years. </p>
      <p>In 1913 she helped <a href="/wiki/Sylvia_Pankhurst" title="Sylvia Pankhurst">Sylvia Pankhurst</a> to evade police capture by disguising herself as Pankhurst.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[167]</a></sup> She was married to <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Postgate" title="Raymond Postgate">Raymond Postgate</a>, the left-wing writer and historian, who was Lansbury's first biographer and founder of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Good_Food_Guide" title="The Good Food Guide">The Good Food Guide</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[168]</a></sup> Their son, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Postgate" title="Oliver Postgate">Oliver Postgate</a>, was a successful writer, animator and producer for children's television.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[169]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Lansbury home at 39 Bow Road was destroyed by bombing during the <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">London Blitz</a> of 1940–41.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[170]</a></sup> There is a small memorial stone dedicated to Lansbury in front of the current building, appropriately named George Lansbury House, which itself carries a memorial plaque. There is also a memorial to Lansbury in the nearby <a href="/wiki/Bow_Church" title="Bow Church">Bow Church</a>, where Lansbury was a long-term member of the congregation and churchwarden. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Books_by_Lansbury">Books by Lansbury</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Books by Lansbury">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <ul>
       <li><cite class="citation book"><i>Your Part in Poverty</i>. London: Allen and Unwin. 1918. <a href="/wiki/OCLC" title="OCLC">OCLC</a>&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/251051169">251051169</a>.</cite><span       <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <ul>
       <li><a href="/wiki/List_of_peace_activists" title="List of peace activists">List of peace activists</a></li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/List_of_suffragists_and_suffragettes" title="List of suffragists and suffragettes">List of suffragists and suffragettes</a></li>
      </ul> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Lansbury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;"> 
       <ol class="references"> 
        <li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lansbury's biographer <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Postgate" title="Raymond Postgate">Raymond Postgate</a> gives the date and place of birth as 21 February, at the toll-house between <a href="/wiki/Halesworth" title="Halesworth">Halesworth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lowestoft" title="Lowestoft">Lowestoft</a>, in the county of <a href="/wiki/Suffolk" title="Suffolk">Suffolk</a>. However, according to his birth certificate, Lansbury was born on 22 February 1859, at a house in Halesworth's "Thoroughfare" or High Street; a plaque provided by a local historical society in 1993 identifies the building as No. 14.<sup id="cite_ref-P3_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P3-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-S5_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S5-3">[3]</a></sup> </span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The English team, managed by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Shaw" title="Alfred Shaw">Alfred Shaw</a>, was in Australia from November 1884 until the end of March 1885, but the tour record shows no matches played at Brisbane.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup></span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At that time women, although denied votes in parliamentary elections, had limited rights to vote in municipal elections, although whether they could stand as candidates, or serve if elected, was not legally clear.<sup id="cite_ref-S63_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S63-24">[22]</a></sup></span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There being no specific trades union for sawmill workers Lansbury had, in 1889, joined the Gas-workers and General Labourers' Union. He remained a member for the remainder of his life, and for many years attended Labour Party conferences as a union rather than a local party delegate.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup></span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1920 Lansbury published a rationale for his Christian beliefs, under the title <i>These Things Shall Be</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[40]</a></sup></span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1907 the school moved to new buildings in <a href="/wiki/Shenfield" title="Shenfield">Shenfield</a>, Essex. By 1974 it had become an adult training centre; many of the original buildings were demolished and rebuilt in the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup></span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1900 the <a href="/wiki/British_Labour_Party#Labour_Representation_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="British Labour Party">Labour Representation Committee</a> (LRC) had been formed to promote greater working class representation in parliament. In 1906 the LRC became a <i>de facto</i> political party, "the Labour Party", to which socialist bodies (SDF, ILP, trades unions) could affiliate; MPs elected under the LRC banner took the label "Labour". The party did not acquire its modern mass-membership nature until reforms under a new constitution were implemented in 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[60]</a></sup> </span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Cat and Mouse Act, officially the <a href="/wiki/Prisoners_(Temporary_Discharge_for_Ill_Health)_Act_1913" title="Prisoners (Temporary Discharge for Ill Health) Act 1913">Prisoners (Temporary Discharge for Ill Health) Act 1913</a>, allowed for the temporary release of hunger-striking prisoners when they were in danger of death from starvation, and their re-imprisonment when they had sufficiently recovered.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[70]</a></sup></span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Henderson" title="Arthur Henderson">Arthur Henderson</a>, who led the parliamentary Labour group between 1914 and 1917, occupied several cabinet posts under Asquith and Lloyd George, and was a member of the latter's small inner war cabinet. Other Labour members with government posts included <a href="/wiki/William_Brace" title="William Brace">William Brace</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Henry_Roberts" class="mw-redirect" title="George Henry Roberts">George Roberts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[79]</a></sup></span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1921 the borough of Poplar, with a population of 161,000, has a rateable value of less than £1 million; the product of a penny rate was £3643. By contrast, the rateable value of the wealthy borough of Westminster, with a population of 141,000, was £8 million, and the product of a penny rate was £31,719.<sup id="cite_ref-P216_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P216-100">[91]</a></sup></span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mosley resigned from the government. He later left the Labour Party and formed the <a href="/wiki/New_Party_(UK)" title="New Party (UK)">New Party</a>, from which developed the <a href="/wiki/British_Union_of_Fascists" title="British Union of Fascists">British Union of Fascists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[122]</a></sup></span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-136">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The May recommendations were for immediate savings of £120 million (a vast sum at the time), of which £24 million would come from increased taxation and £96 million by expenditure cuts of which the largest proportion would come from unemployment relief. The economist <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a> called the May report "the most foolish document I ever had the misfortune to read".<sup id="cite_ref-T362_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T362-135">[124]</a></sup></span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-139"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-139">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Taylor gives the 1931 election result as National Government 521 (Conservatives, National Labour and National Liberals); opposition: Labour 52, Liberal 33, Lloyd George family 4. The Labour total of 52 included 6 ILP members who disaffiliated from the Labour Party in 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-T404_131-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T404-131">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[126]</a></sup></span> </li> 
       </ol>
      </div>
